Given the different types of qualitative evidence available in support of individual modes of transmission (Table [1](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#Tab1)), discussion may be warranted in assigning priority or strength of evidence to these different types of evidence in support of each mode; for example, whether the demonstration of transmission via aerosols alone in the absence of other routes is essential to support the importance of the aerosol route, as suggested for measles virus[57](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR57 "Bloch, A. B. et al. Measles outbreak in a pediatric practice: airborne transmission in an office setting. Pediatrics 75, 676–683 (1985).") and VZV[166](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR166 "Leclair, J. M., Zaia, J. A., Levin, M. J., Congdon, R. G. & Goldmann, D. A. Airborne transmission of chickenpox in a hospital. N. Engl. J. Med. 302, 450–453 (1980)."), but which was not done in support of the importance of the droplet route for RSV. These study designs may be possible for aerosols and fomites, but it will be more challenging to demonstrate transmission via direct contact and droplets in the absence of (close-range) aerosols. For rhinovirus, airborne precautions are not required despite the evidence of aerosol transmission in the absence of other routes[63](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR63 "Couch, R. B., Cate, T. R., Douglas, R. G., Gerone, P. J. & Knight, V. Effect of route of inoculation on experimental respiratory viral disease in volunteers and evidence for airborne transmission. Bacteriol. Rev. 30, 517–529 (1966)."), [117](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR117 "Dick, E. C., Jennings, L. C., Mink, K. A., Wartgow, C. D. & Inhorn, S. L. Aerosol transmission of rhinovirus colds. J. Infect. Dis. 156, 442–448 (1987)."); in addition, as rhinovirus transmission has been demonstrated independently via direct contact[202](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR202 "Gwaltney, J. M., Jr. Moskalski, P. B. & Hendley, J. O. Interruption of experimental rhinovirus transmission. J. Infect. Dis. 142, 811–815 (1980)."), fomite[109](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR109 "Gwaltney, J. M. & Hendley, J. O. Transmission of experimental rhinovirus infection by contaminated surfaces. Am. J. Epidemiol. 116, 828–833 (1982).") and aerosols[63](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR63 "Couch, R. B., Cate, T. R., Douglas, R. G., Gerone, P. J. & Knight, V. Effect of route of inoculation on experimental respiratory viral disease in volunteers and evidence for airborne transmission. Bacteriol. Rev. 30, 517–529 (1966)."), [117](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR117 "Dick, E. C., Jennings, L. C., Mink, K. A., Wartgow, C. D. & Inhorn, S. L. Aerosol transmission of rhinovirus colds. J. Infect. Dis. 156, 442–448 (1987)."), efforts should be made to quantitatively evaluate the relative contribution of each mode to transmission and their determinants in different settings[130](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR130 "Jones, R. M. & Adida, E. Influenza infection risk and predominate exposure route: uncertainty analysis. Risk Anal. 31, 1622–1631 (2011)."). However, the relative contribution of different modes at the population level likely varies between different settings, populations and interventions in place, and at the individual level varies between individuals due to heterogeneity in contagiousness and susceptibility, which also changes over time. There is also minimal research on the modes of transmission of bocavirus and metapneumovirus (Supplementary Table [1](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#MOESM1)). In general, there is a lack of studies demonstrating droplet transmission alone in the absence of other routes for all respiratory viruses (Table [1](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#Tab1)), and such studies are urgently needed to support the importance of droplets over aerosols and will have important implications for the choice of NPIs for mitigating transmission. Alternatively, aerosol transmission does not necessarily indicate a higher intrinsic transmissibility of the virus (Table [1](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#Tab1)) nor long-range transmission, as transmissibility depends on multiple factors, including the degree of presymptomatic transmission, the contagiousness of the infector, the susceptibility of the infectee, the contact patterns between them and the environmental determinants of transmission in the shared space; and long-range transmission depends on rates of virus emission at the source or effective dilution or inactivation by environmental determinants. Moving beyond deciding on the adoption of an NPI mostly on the basis of the perceived importance of a particular mode of transmission would provide an incentive for public health practitioners to recognize the importance of aerosol transmission.

For common NPIs, although we have mechanistic evidence supporting their efficacy with regard to virus reduction or inactivation, we have limited knowledge of their effectiveness in reducing transmission in the population both in health-care settings and in community settings. This may be because we do not yet know the relative contribution of different modes of transmission in a particular setting and whether the different modes can partially compensate for each other if a mode is absent[174](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR174 "Cowling, B. J. et al. Aerosol transmission is an important mode of influenza A virus spread. Nat. Commun. 4, 1935 (2013)."). If the latter is true, studies evaluating the effectiveness of one intervention alone, which targets a specific mode, may underestimate its potential effectiveness because a reduction in transmission via a specific mode by the NPI might be compensated by transmission via another mode[174](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR174 "Cowling, B. J. et al. Aerosol transmission is an important mode of influenza A virus spread. Nat. Commun. 4, 1935 (2013)."). Given effectiveness is demonstrated, the eventual adoption of NPIs would also depend on the perceived severity of the disease[15](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR15 "Shiu, E. Y. C., Leung, N. H. L. & Cowling, B. J. Controversy around airborne versus droplet transmission of respiratory viruses: implication for infection prevention. Curr. Opin. Infect. Dis. 32, 372–379 (2019)."), the infection risk in a particular setting[175](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR175 "World Health Organization. Infection prevention and control during health care when novel coronavirus (nCoV) infection is suspected: interim guidance (March 2020).                    https://www.who.int/publications/i/item/10665-331495                                     (2020)."), [180](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR180 "Chu, D. K. et al. Physical distancing, face masks, and eye protection to prevent person-to-person transmission of SARS-CoV-2 and COVID-19: a systematic review and meta-analysis. Lancet 395, 1973–1987 (2020)."), the accessibility of the resources, the purpose of interventions[6](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR6 "Brankston, G., Gitterman, L., Hirji, Z., Lemieux, C. & Gardam, M. Transmission of influenza A in human beings. Lancet Infect. Dis. 7, 257–265 (2007).") and the economic or societal costs of implementing the intervention. In particular, the choice of which transmission-based precautions to adopt for a particular respiratory virus in health-care settings depends on the perceived major modes of transmission for the pathogen[15](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR15 "Shiu, E. Y. C., Leung, N. H. L. & Cowling, B. J. Controversy around airborne versus droplet transmission of respiratory viruses: implication for infection prevention. Curr. Opin. Infect. Dis. 32, 372–379 (2019)."), the level of caution and the resources likely available if the recommendation is made[15](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR15 "Shiu, E. Y. C., Leung, N. H. L. & Cowling, B. J. Controversy around airborne versus droplet transmission of respiratory viruses: implication for infection prevention. Curr. Opin. Infect. Dis. 32, 372–379 (2019)."), and therefore could differ between countries. Intervening against multiple modes of transmission would be more effective than acting against a single mode. For example, although effectiveness of the use of face masks or hand hygiene alone in mitigating community transmission of laboratory-confirmed respiratory virus infection was not demonstrated, possibly due to various experimental challenges, their combined use has been shown to be effective in reducing influenza virus transmission and should be considered[203](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41579-021-00535-6#ref-CR203 "Wong, V. W., Cowling, B. J. & Aiello, A. E. Hand hygiene and risk of influenza virus infections in the community: a systematic review and meta-analysis. Epidemiol. Infect. 142, 922–932 (2014)."). A clear public health message accounting for these uncertainties will help to gain public confidence and support public health efforts.
